             <a href="/wiki/Philippine_dance" title="Philippine dance">
              Performing arts
             </a>
            </li>
           </ul>
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>

       <tr>
        <th style="padding:0.2em 0.4em 0.2em;font-size:145%;line-height:1.2em;background:lavender">

         <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
          Philippines
         </a>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg">
          <img alt="Nuvola Philippines flag.svg" data-file-height="60" data-file-width="60" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg/50px-Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg/75px-Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg/100px-Nuvola_Philippines_flag.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </a>

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Life_in_the_Philippines" title="Template:Life in the Philippines">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Life_in_the_Philippines" title="Template talk:Life in the Philippines">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Life_in_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       There are some 120 to 187
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       languages and dialects in the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
        Philippines
       </a>
       , depending on the method of classification.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Almost all are
       <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">
        Malayo-Polynesian languages
       </a>
       , whereas one,
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
       , is a
       <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">
        creole
       </a>
       derived from
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">
        Romance language
       </a>
       . Two, English and Filipino, are official, while nineteen (as of 2017
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) are official
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_regional_languages_in_the_Philippines" title="List of regional languages in the Philippines">
        auxiliary languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7_mother_tounges_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-7_mother_tounges-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Including second-language speakers, there are more speakers of Filipino than English in the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komisyon_sa_Wikang_Filipino" title="Komisyon sa Wikang Filipino">
        Komisyon sa Wikang Filipino
       </a>
       enumerated 130 Philippine languages present in the country through its Atlas Filipinas map published in 2016.
      </p>
      <p>
       The indigenous scripts of the Philippines (such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Kulitan_alphabet" title="Kulitan alphabet">
        Kulitan
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagbanwa_alphabet" title="Tagbanwa alphabet">
        Tagbanwa
       </a>
       and others) are used very little; instead, Filipino languages are today written in the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">
        Latin script
       </a>
       because of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Philippines" title="Spanish Philippines">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1898%E2%80%931946)" title="History of the Philippines (1898–1946)">
        American colonial experience
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Baybayin" title="Baybayin">
        Baybayin
       </a>
       , however, one of the most well-known of the indigenous Filipino scripts, is used by the government in some applications such as on the
       <a href="/wiki/Banknotes_of_the_Philippine_peso" title="Banknotes of the Philippine peso">
        Philippine banknotes
       </a>
       , where the word "Pilipino" is inscribed using the writing system. Additionally, the
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_script" title="Arabic script">
        Arabic script
       </a>
       is used in the
       <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_Region_in_Muslim_Mindanao" title="Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao">
        Muslim areas
       </a>
       in the southern Philippines.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="National_and_official_languages">
        National and official languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: National and official languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1987_Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="1987 Constitution of the Philippines">
        1987 Constitution
       </a>
       declares
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
        Filipino
       </a>
       as the national language of the country.
       <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
        Filipino
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">
        English
       </a>
       are the official languages, with the recognition of the regional languages as auxiliary official in their respective regions, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aklan_language" title="Aklan language">
        Aklanon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Bikol_language" title="Central Bikol language">
        Bikol
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
        Cebuano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hiligaynon_language" title="Hiligaynon language">
        Hiligaynon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ibanag_language" title="Ibanag language">
        Ibanag
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">
        Ilocano
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ivatan_language" title="Ivatan language">
        Ivatan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">
        Kapampangan
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinaray-a_language" title="Kinaray-a language">
        Kinaray-a
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Maguindanao_language" title="Maguindanao language">
        Maguindanao
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Maranao_language" title="Maranao language">
        Maranao
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pangasinan_language" title="Pangasinan language">
        Pangasinan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sambal_language" title="Sambal language">
        Sambal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Surigaonon_language" title="Surigaonon language">
        Surigaonon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
        Tagalog
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tausug_language" title="Tausug language">
        Tausug
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Waray_language" title="Waray language">
        Waray
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Yakan_language" title="Yakan language">
        Yakan
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       are to be promoted on an optional and voluntary basis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       was the national and official language of the country for more than three centuries under Spanish colonial rule, and became the
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       of the Philippines in the 19th and early 20th centuries. In 1863, a Spanish decree introduced
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universal_education" title="Universal education">
        universal education
       </a>
       , creating free public schooling in
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhilCS-Edu_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhilCS-Edu-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was also the language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
       , and the 1899
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        Malolos Constitution
       </a>
       effectively proclaimed it as the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/First_Philippine_Republic" title="First Philippine Republic">
        First Philippine Republic
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       National hero
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Rizal" title="José Rizal">
        José Rizal
       </a>
       wrote most of his works in Spanish. Luciano de la Rosa established that Spanish was spoken by a total of 60% of the population in the early 20th century as a first, second or third language. Following the American occupation of the Philippines and the imposition of English, the use of Spanish declined gradually, especially after the 1940s.
      </p>
      <p>
       Under the U.S. occupation and civil regime,
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       began to be taught in schools. By 1901, public education used English as the medium of instruction. Around 600 educators (called "
       <a href="/wiki/Thomasites" title="Thomasites">
        Thomasites
       </a>
       ") who arrived in that year aboard the
       <a href="/wiki/USAT_Thomas" title="USAT Thomas">
        USAT
        <i>
         Thomas
        </i>
       </a>
       replaced the soldiers who also functioned as teachers. The 1935 Constitution added English as an official language alongside Spanish. A provision in this constitution also called for Congress to "take steps toward the development and adoption of a common national language based on one of the existing native languages." On November 12, 1937, the First National Assembly created the National Language Institute. President
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quez%C3%B3n" title="Manuel L. Quezón">
        Manuel L. Quezón
       </a>
       appointed native
       <a href="/wiki/Waray_language" title="Waray language">
        Waray
       </a>
       speaker Jaime C. De Veyra to chair a committee of speakers of other
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        regional languages
       </a>
       . Their aim was to select a national language among the other
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        regional languages
       </a>
       . Ultimately,
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
        Tagalog
       </a>
       was chosen as the base language December 30, 1937, on the basis that it was the most widely spoken and developed local language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quezón1937_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Quezón1937-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1939,
       <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Philippines" title="President of the Philippines">
        President
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manuel_L._Quez%C3%B3n" title="Manuel L. Quezón">
        Manuel L. Quezón
       </a>
       renamed the
       <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
        Tagalog language
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        Wikang Pambansa
       </i>
       ("national language" in English translation).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez1998_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez1998-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The language was further renamed in 1959 as
       <i>
        Pilipino
       </i>
       by Secretary of Education Jose Romero. The 1973 constitution declared the Pilipino language to be co-official, along with English, and mandated the development of a
       <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">
        national language
       </a>
       , to be known as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">
         Filipino
        </a>
       </i>
       . In addition, Spanish regained its official status when President Marcos signed Presidential Decree No. 155, s. 1973.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD_No._155_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-PD_No._155-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The present constitution, ratified in 1987, designates Filipino and English as joint official languages. Filipino also had the distinction of being a national language that was to be "developed and enriched on the basis of existing Philippine and other languages." Although not explicitly stated in the constitution, Filipino is in practice almost completely composed of the Tagalog language as spoken in the capital, Manila; however, organizations such as the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Philippines" title="University of the Philippines">
        University of the Philippines
       </a>
       began publishing dictionaries such as the
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UP_Diksyonaryong_Filipino" title="UP Diksyonaryong Filipino">
         UP Diksyonaryong Filipino
        </a>
       </i>
       in which words from various Philippine languages were also included. The constitution also made mention of Spanish and
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       , both of which are to be promoted on a voluntary and optional basis.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Major_Philippine_languages.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="2000" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Major_Philippine_languages.png/200px-Major_Philippine_languages.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Major_Philippine_languages.png/300px-Major_Philippine_languages.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Major_Philippine_languages.png/400px-Major_Philippine_languages.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Major_Philippine_languages.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Language map of the 12 recognized auxiliary languages based on
         <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
          Ethnologue
         </a>
         maps.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Filipino is an official language of education and also the major language of the broadcast
       <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">
        media
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">
        cinema
       </a>
       , but less important than English as a language of publication (except in some domains, like
       <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">
        comic books
       </a>
       ) and less important for academic-scientific-technological discourse. Filipino is used as a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       in all regions of the Philippines as well as within
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Overseas_Filipino" title="Overseas Filipino">
        overseas Filipino
       </a>
       communities, and is the dominant language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_the_Philippines" title="Armed Forces of the Philippines">
        armed forces
       </a>
       (except perhaps for the small part of the commissioned
       <a href="/wiki/Officer_(armed_forces)" title="Officer (armed forces)">
        officer
       </a>
       corps from wealthy or upper-middle-class families) and of a large part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">
        civil service
       </a>
       , most of whom are non-Tagalogs.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are different forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
        diglossia
       </a>
       that exist in the case of
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        regional languages
       </a>
       . Locals may use their mother tongue or the regional
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       to communicate amongst themselves, but sometimes switch to foreign languages when addressing outsiders. Another is the prevalence of
       <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">
        code-switching
       </a>
       to English when speaking in both their first language and Tagalog.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="Constitution of the Philippines">
        Constitution of the Philippines
       </a>
       provides for the use of the vernacular languages as official
       <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">
        auxiliary languages
       </a>
       in provinces where Filipino is not the
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       . This is however not implemented as Filipinos at large are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyglot_(person)" title="Polyglot (person)">
        polyglots
       </a>
       . In the case where the vernacular language is a regional language, Filipinos would speak in Filipino when speaking in formal situations while the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        regional languages
       </a>
       are spoken in non-formal settings. This is evident in major urban areas outside Metro Manila like
       <a href="/wiki/Camarines_Norte" title="Camarines Norte">
        Camarines Norte
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bikol_language" title="Bikol language">
        Bikol
       </a>
       -speaking area, and
       <a href="/wiki/Davao_City" title="Davao City">
        Davao
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
        Cebuano
       </a>
       -speaking area. As of 2017
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the case of Ilocano and Cebuano are becoming more of bilingualism than diglossia due to the publication of materials written in these languages.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The diglossia is more evident in the case of other languages such as
       <a href="/wiki/Pangasinan_language" title="Pangasinan language">
        Pangasinan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">
        Kapampangan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
        Bikol
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Waray_language" title="Waray language">
        Waray
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hiligaynon_language" title="Hiligaynon language">
        Hiligaynon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sambal_language" title="Sambal language">
        Sambal
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Maranao_language" title="Maranao language">
        Maranao
       </a>
       , where the written variant of the language is becoming less and less popular to give way to the use of Filipino. Although Philippine laws consider some of these languages as "major languages" there is little, if any, support coming from the government to preserve these languages. This may be bound to change, however, given current policy trends.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There still exists another type of diglossia, which is between the regional languages and the minority languages. Here, we label the regional languages as acrolects while the minority languages as the basilect. In this case, the minority language is spoken only in very intimate circles, like the family or the tribe one belongs to. Outside this circle, one would speak in the prevalent regional language, while maintaining an adequate command of Filipino for formal situations. Unlike the case of the regional languages, these minority languages are always in danger of becoming extinct because of speakers favoring the more prevalent regional language. Moreover, most of the users of these languages are illiterate
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">
          <span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (November 2011)">
           specify
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       and as expected, there is a chance that these languages will no longer be revived due to lack of written records.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_languages">
        Indigenous languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Indigenous languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philippine_languages_per_region.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3120" data-file-width="2000" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Philippine_languages_per_region.png/200px-Philippine_languages_per_region.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Philippine_languages_per_region.png/300px-Philippine_languages_per_region.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Philippine_languages_per_region.png/400px-Philippine_languages_per_region.png 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philippine_languages_per_region.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Major languages by region. Regions marked with black diamonds denote the language comprises only a substantial minority of the populace.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
        Ethnologue
       </a>
       , a total of 182 native languages are spoken in the nation and four languages have been classified as extinct: Dicamay Agta, Katabaga,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tayabas_Ayta_language" title="Tayabas Ayta language">
        Tayabas Ayta
       </a>
       and Villaviciosa Agta.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ethnologue_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ethnologue-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Except for
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">
        varieties of Chinese
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">
        Hokkien
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">
        Cantonese
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandarin_dialects" title="Mandarin dialects">
        Mandarin
       </a>
       ), all of the languages belong to the
       <a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">
        Malayo-Polynesian language family
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">
        Austronesian superfamily
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There are 13 indigenous languages with at least one million native speakers:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
         Tagalog
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
         Cebuano
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Ilocano_language" title="Ilocano language">
         Ilocano
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Hiligaynon_language" title="Hiligaynon language">
         Hiligaynon
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waray-Waray_language" title="Waray-Waray language">
         Waray-Waray
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Central_Bikol_language" title="Central Bikol language">
         Bikol
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Kapampangan_language" title="Kapampangan language">
         Kapampangan
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Pangasinan_language" title="Pangasinan language">
         Pangasinan
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Maranao_language" title="Maranao language">
         Maranao
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Tausug_language" title="Tausug language">
         Tausug
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Maguindanao_language" title="Maguindanao language">
         Maguindanao
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinaray-a_language" title="Kinaray-a language">
         Kinaray-a
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       One or more of these is spoken natively by more than 90% of the population.
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_languages" title="Philippine languages">
        Philippine language sub-family
       </a>
       identified by
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Blust" title="Robert Blust">
        Robert Blust
       </a>
       includes languages of north
       <a href="/wiki/Sulawesi" title="Sulawesi">
        Sulawesi
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Yami_language" title="Yami language">
        Yami language
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Taiwan
       </a>
       , but excludes the
       <a href="/wiki/Sama%E2%80%93Bajaw_languages" title="Sama–Bajaw languages">
        Sama–Bajaw languages
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Tawi-Tawi" title="Tawi-Tawi">
        Tawi-Tawi
       </a>
       islands, as well as a couple of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Bornean_languages" title="North Bornean languages">
        North Bornean languages
       </a>
       spoken in southern
       <a href="/wiki/Palawan" title="Palawan">
        Palawan
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Eskayan_language" title="Eskayan language">
        Eskayan
       </a>
       is an artificial auxiliary language created as the embodiment of a
       <a href="/wiki/Bohol" title="Bohol">
        Bohol
       </a>
       nation in the aftermath of the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine%E2%80%93American_War" title="Philippine–American War">
        Philippine–American War
       </a>
       . It is used by about 500 people.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mutual_intelligibility">
        Mutual intelligibility
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Mutual intelligibility">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Philippine languages are often referred to by Filipinos as
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialects
       </a>
       , partly as a relic of the inaccurate vocabulary used in literature during the American period (1898–1946).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez1998_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez1998-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While there are indeed many hundreds of dialects in the Philippines, they represent variations of no fewer than 120 distinct languages, and many of these languages maintain greater differences than those between established European languages like French and Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       The vast differences between the languages can be seen in the following translations of the
       <a class="external text" href="http://iloko.tripod.com/philproverb.html" rel="nofollow">
        Philippine national proverb
       </a>
       :
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable sortable" style="font-size:95%;background-color:white;">
       <tr>
        <th scope="col">
         Language
        </th>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialectal_variation">
        Dialectal variation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dialectal variation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The amount of dialectal variation varies from language to language. Languages like Tagalog, Kapampangan and Pangasinan are known to have very moderate dialectal variation.
      </p>
      <p>
       The languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Bicol_Region" title="Bicol Region">
        Bicol Region
       </a>
       , however, there is great dialectal variation. There are cities and towns which have their own dialects and varieties. Below is the sentence "Were you there at the market for a long time?" translated into certain varieties of Bikol. The translation is followed by dialect and corresponding
       <a href="/wiki/Bikol_languages" title="Bikol languages">
        language
       </a>
       , and a city/town in Bicol where they are spoken. The final translation is in Tagalog.
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Haloy ka duman sa saodan? (Standard Coastal Bikol, a dialect of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Bikol" title="Central Bikol">
         Central Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Canaman,_Camarines_Sur" title="Canaman, Camarines Sur">
         Canaman, Camarines Sur
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Aloy ka duman sa saodan? (Magarao, a variety of Coastal Bikol;
        <a href="/wiki/Magarao,_Camarines_Sur" title="Magarao, Camarines Sur">
         Magarao, Camarines Sur
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Huray ka doon sa saodan? (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pandan_Bikol" title="Pandan Bikol">
         Northern Catanduanes Bicolano or
         <i>
          Pandan Bikol
         </i>
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Pandan,_Catanduanes" title="Pandan, Catanduanes">
         Pandan, Catanduanes
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Naәban ikā sadtō sāran? (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rinconada_Bikol" title="Rinconada Bikol">
         Rinconada Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iriga_City" title="Iriga City">
         Iriga City
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Nauban ikā sadtō sāran? (
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rinconada_Bikol" title="Rinconada Bikol">
         Rinconada Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Nabua,_Camarines_Sur" title="Nabua, Camarines Sur">
         Nabua, Camarines Sur
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Uban ika adto sa saod? (Libon,
        <a href="/wiki/Albay_Bikol_language" title="Albay Bikol language">
         Albay Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Libon,_Albay" title="Libon, Albay">
         Libon, Albay
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Naәgәy ika adto sa saran? (Buhinon,
        <a href="/wiki/Albay_Bikol_language" title="Albay Bikol language">
         Albay Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Buhi,_Camarines_Sur" title="Buhi, Camarines Sur">
         Buhi, Camarines Sur
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Eley ka idto sa sed? (West Miraya Bikol,
        <a href="/wiki/Albay_Bikol_language" title="Albay Bikol language">
         Albay Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Oas,_Albay" title="Oas, Albay">
         Oas, Albay
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Na-alõy ika idto sa sâran/mercado?(West Miraya Bikol,
        <a href="/wiki/Albay_Bikol_language" title="Albay Bikol language">
         Albay Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Polangui,_Albay" title="Polangui, Albay">
         Polangui, Albay
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Naulay ka didto sa saran? (East Miraya Bikol,
        <a href="/wiki/Albay_Bikol_language" title="Albay Bikol language">
         Albay Bikol
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Daraga,_Albay" title="Daraga, Albay">
         Daraga, Albay
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Dugay ka didto sa mercado? (Ticao,
        <a href="/wiki/Masbate%C3%B1o_language" title="Masbateño language">
         Masbateño
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Monreal,_Masbate" title="Monreal, Masbate">
         Monreal, Masbate
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Awat ka didto sa plasa? (Gubat,
        <a href="/wiki/Sorsoganon_language" title="Sorsoganon language">
         Southern Sorsogon
        </a>
        ;
        <a href="/wiki/Gubat,_Sorsogon" title="Gubat, Sorsogon">
         Gubat, Sorsogon
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        Matagal ka ba roon sa pamilihan? (
        <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">
         Tagalog
        </a>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_chart">
        Comparison chart
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Comparison chart">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Below is a chart of Philippine languages. While there have been misunderstandings on which ones should be classified as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
         language
        </a>
       </i>
       and which ones should be classified as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
         dialect
        </a>
       </i>
       , the chart confirms that most have similarities, yet are not mutually comprehensible. These languages are arranged according to the regions they are natively spoken (from north to south, then east to west).
      </p>

         <span class="nowrap">
          Caviteño: nisos
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         cosá/ qué
        </td>
        <td>
         y/e
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       There is a language spoken by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tao_people" title="Tao people">
        Tao people
       </a>
       (also known as Yami) of
       <a href="/wiki/Orchid_Island" title="Orchid Island">
        Orchid Island
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Taiwan
       </a>
       which is not included in the language of the Philippines. Their language,
       <a href="/wiki/Yami_language" title="Yami language">
        Tao
       </a>
       (or Yami) is part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Batanic_languages" title="Batanic languages">
        Batanic languages
       </a>
       which includes
       <a href="/wiki/Ivatan_language" title="Ivatan language">
        Ivatan
       </a>
       , Babuyan, and Itbayat of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Batanes" title="Batanes">
         Batanes
        </a>
       </i>
       .
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_vitality">
        Language vitality
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Language vitality">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2010_UNESCO_designation">
        2010 UNESCO designation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2010 UNESCO designation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Endangered and extinct languages in the Philippines are based on the 3rd world volume released by UNESCO in 2010.
      </p>
      <p>
       Degree of endangerment (UNESCO standard)
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Safe
        </i>
        : language is spoken by all generations; intergenerational transmission is uninterrupted
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Vulnerable
        </i>
        : most children speak the language, but it may be restricted to certain domains (e.g., home)
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Definitely endangered
        </i>
        : children no longer learn the language as mother tongue in the home
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Severely endangered
        </i>
        : language is spoken by grandparents and older generations; while the parent generation may understand it, they do not speak it to children or among themselves
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Critically endangered
        </i>
        : the youngest speakers are grandparents and older, and they speak the language partially and infrequently
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Extinct
        </i>
        : there are no speakers left &gt;&gt; included in the Atlas if presumably extinct since the 1950s
       </li>
      </ul>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Vulnerable languages
       </dt>
      </dl>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2014_North_Dakota_study">
        2014 North Dakota study
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2014 North Dakota study">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In a separate study by Thomas N. Headland, the Summer Institute of Linguistics in Dallas, and the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Dakota" title="University of North Dakota">
        University of North Dakota
       </a>
       called
       <i>
        Thirty Endangered Languages in the Philippines
       </i>
       , the Philippines has 32 endangered languages, but 2 of the listed languages in the study are written with 0 speakers, noting that they are extinct or probably extinct. All of the listed languages are Negrito languages, the oldest languages in the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endangered_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-endangered-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="font-size:95%;background-color:white;">
       <tr style="background-color:#ffdead;">
        <th scope="col" style="background-color:initial;">
         Language
        </th>
        <th scope="col" style="background-color:initial;">
         General location
         <br/>
         of speakers
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endangered_19-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-endangered-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th scope="col" style="background-color:initial;">
         Population of speakers
         <br/>
         in the 1990s
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endangered_19-2">
          <a href="#cite_note-endangered-19">
           [19]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th scope="col" style="background-color:initial;">
         Bibliographic source
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-endangered_19-3">
          <a href="#cite_note-endangered-19">

         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mt._Iraya" title="Mt. Iraya">

         </a>

         <a href="/wiki/Lake_Buhi" title="Lake Buhi">

         </a>
         east, Camarines Sur (has 4 close dialects)
        </td>

        </td>
