      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proposals_to_Conserve_Philippine_Languages">
        Proposals to Conserve Philippine Languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Proposals to Conserve Philippine Languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There have been numerous proposals to conserve the many languages of the Philippines. According to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komisyon_ng_Wikang_Filipino" title="Komisyon ng Wikang Filipino">
        Komisyon ng Wikang Filipino
       </a>
       , there are 135 ethno-linguistic groups in the country, each having their own distinct Philippine language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Among the proposals include (1)
       <i>
        "establishing a dictionary &amp; sentence construction manual"
       </i>
       for each of the 135 living languages in the country, (2)
       <i>
        "video documentation"
       </i>
       of all Philippine languages, (3)
       <i>
        "revival of the ancient scripts of the Philippines"
       </i>
       where each ethnic group's own script shall be revived and used in schools along with the currently-used Roman script in communities where those script/s used to be known, (4)
       <i>
        "teaching of ethnic mother languages first"
       </i>
       in homes and schools before the teaching of Filipino and foreign languages (English or Arabic), and (5)
       <i>
        "using the ethnic mother language and script first in public signs"
       </i>
       followed by Filipino and foreign languages (English and/or Arabic) and scripts, for example, using Sebwano first followed by Filipino and English underneath the sign.
      </p>
      <p>
       Currently, only the fourth proposal has been made by the national government of the Philippines.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A National Script bill has been filed in Congress in support of the third and fifth proposal, however, the bill only mandates the usage of the ancient script compatible with the national language, which is Filipino.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Major_immigrant_languages">
        Major immigrant languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Major immigrant languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Arabic">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Arabic is used by some
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filipino_Muslim" title="Filipino Muslim">
        Filipino Muslims
       </a>
       in both a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" title="Liturgical language">
        liturgical
       </a>
       and instructional capacity since the arrival of
       <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_Philippines" title="Islam in the Philippines">
        Islam
       </a>
       and establishment of several Sultanates in the 14th century. Along with
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
        Malay
       </a>
       , Arabic was the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
       </i>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_Archipelago" title="Malay Archipelago">
        Malay Archipelago
       </a>
       among Muslim traders and the Malay
       <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">
        aristocracy
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1987_Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="1987 Constitution of the Philippines">
        1987 Constitution
       </a>
       mandates that Arabic (along with Spanish) is to be promoted on an optional and voluntary basis. As of 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Arabic is taught for free and is promoted in some Islamic centres predominantly in the southernmost parts of Philippines. It is used primarily in religious activities and education (such as in a
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">
         madrasa
        </a>
       </i>
       or Islamic school) and rarely for official events or daily conversation. In this respect, its function and use is somewhat like the traditional roles of
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       and Spanish in
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_the_Philippines" title="Catholic Church in the Philippines">
        Filipino Catholicism
       </a>
       <i>
        vis-à-vis
       </i>
       other currently spoken languages.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="English">
        English
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: English">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">
        Philippine English
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Taglish" title="Taglish">
        Taglish
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Bislish" title="Bislish">
        Bislish
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first significant exposure of Filipinos to the
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English language
       </a>
       occurred in 1762 when the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Occupation_of_Manila" title="British Occupation of Manila">
        British invaded Manila
       </a>
       , but this was a brief episode that had no lasting influence. English later became more important and widespread during
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Colonial_Period_(Philippines)" title="American Colonial Period (Philippines)">
        American rule
       </a>
       between 1898 and 1946, and remains an
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       of the Philippines.
      </p>
      <p>
       English is used in official documents of business, government, the legal system, medicine, the sciences and as a medium of instruction. Filipinos prefer textbooks for subjects like calculus, physics, chemistry, biology, etc., written in English rather than Filipino.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (December 2015)">
           dubious
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Languages_of_the_Philippines#Dubious" title="Talk:Languages of the Philippines">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       However, the topics are usually taught, even in colleges, in Tagalog or the local language. By way of contrast, native languages are often heard in colloquial and domestic settings, spoken mostly with family and friends. The use of English attempts to give an air of formality, given its use in school, government and various ceremonies.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="Reliable sources needed for the past 4 sentences (October 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       A percentage of the media such as cable television and newspapers are also in English; major television networks such as
       <a href="/wiki/ABS-CBN" title="ABS-CBN">
        ABS-CBN
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/GMA_Network" title="GMA Network">
        GMA
       </a>
       and all AM radio stations broadcast primarily in Filipino. However, a 2009 article by a
       <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">
        UNICEF
       </a>
       worker reported that the level of spoken English language in the Philippines was poor. The article reported that aspiring Filipino teachers score the lowest in English out of all of the subjects on their licensing exams.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDO2009-01-21_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SDO2009-01-21-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A large influx of English (American English) words has been assimilated into Tagalog and the other native languages called
       <a href="/wiki/Taglish" title="Taglish">
        Taglish
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Bislish" title="Bislish">
        Bislish
       </a>
       . There is a debate, however, on whether there is diglossia or bilingualism, between Filipino and English. Filipino is also used both in formal and informal situations. Though the masses would prefer to speak in Filipino, government officials tend to speak in English when performing government functions.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
          <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (October 2013)">
           according to whom?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       There is still resistance to the use of Filipino in courts and the drafting of national statutes.
      </p>
      <p>
       On August 22, 2007, three
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malolos_City" title="Malolos City">
        Malolos City
       </a>
       regional trial courts in
       <a href="/wiki/Bulacan" title="Bulacan">
        Bulacan
       </a>
       decided to use Filipino, instead of
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_English" title="Philippine English">
        English
       </a>
       , in order to promote the national language. Twelve
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stenographers" title="Stenographers">
        stenographers
       </a>
       from Branches 6, 80 and 81, as model courts, had undergone training at Marcelo H. del Pilar College of Law of
       <a href="/wiki/Bulacan_State_University" title="Bulacan State University">
        Bulacan State University
       </a>
       College of Law following a directive from the
       <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_Philippines" title="Supreme Court of the Philippines">
        Supreme Court of the Philippines
       </a>
       . De la Rama said it was the dream of former Chief Justice
       <a href="/wiki/Reynato_Puno" title="Reynato Puno">
        Reynato Puno
       </a>
       to implement the program in other areas such as
       <a href="/wiki/Laguna_(province)" title="Laguna (province)">
        Laguna
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cavite" title="Cavite">
        Cavite
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quezon" title="Quezon">
        Quezón
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nueva_%C3%89cija" title="Nueva Écija">
        Nueva Écija
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Batangas" title="Batangas">
        Batangas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rizal" title="Rizal">
        Rizal
       </a>
       , and Metro Manila.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hokkien">
        Hokkien
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hokkien">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Hokkien" title="Philippine Hokkien">
        Philippine Hokkien
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Diplomatic ties with the Ming dynasty among some established states or kingdoms in Luzon and direct interactions and trade overall within the archipelago as a whole may go as far back as the early 10th century.
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Mandarin Chinese
       </a>
       is the medium of instruction in Chinese schools and the lingua franca of mainland and overseas Chinese. The Lan-nang variant of
       <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">
        Hokkien
       </a>
       Chinese is the language of the majority the Chinese in the Philippines, who immigrated from the
       <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">
        Fujian
       </a>
       (pronounced locally as Fukien or Hokkien) province in China. Other
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">
        varieties of Chinese
       </a>
       such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">
        Hakka
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">
        Cantonese
       </a>
       , are spoken among the Chinese in the Philippines who are descendants of people from
       <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">
        Guangdong
       </a>
       province in China.
      </p>
      <p>
       As with Spanish, many native languages have co-opted numerous
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_loanwords_in_Tagalog#Chinese" title="List of loanwords in Tagalog">
        loanwords from Chinese
       </a>
       , in particular words that refer to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philippine_cuisine" title="Philippine cuisine">
        cuisine
       </a>
       , household objects, and
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_kinship" title="Philippine kinship">
        Philippine kinship
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Kinship_terminology" title="Kinship terminology">
        terminology
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">
        Japanese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Japanese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Japanese first came to the Philippines around the 11th century CE, the first country they emigrated to, as well as in waves from the 15th century, 17th century, late 19th century, 1900s, 1930s, and the 1940s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-almanac_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-almanac-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is a small Japanese community and a school for Japanese in Metro Manila due to the number of Japanese companies. Also there is a large community of Japanese and Japanese descendants in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laguna_province" title="Laguna province">
        Laguna province
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baguio_City" title="Baguio City">
        Baguio City
       </a>
       , and in the
       <a href="/wiki/Davao_Region" title="Davao Region">
        Davao Region
       </a>
       . Davao City is a home to a large population of Japanese descendants. Japanese laborers were hired by American companies like the National Fiber Company (NAFCO) in the first decades of the 20th century to work in abaca plantations. Japanese were known for their hard work and industry. During World War II, Japanese schools were present in Davao City.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Malay">
        Malay
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Malay">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language_in_the_Philippines" title="Malay language in the Philippines">
        Malay language in the Philippines
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Malay is spoken as a lingua franca in the southernmost parts of the Philippines, from
       <a href="/wiki/Zamboanga_Peninsula_(geographical_region)" title="Zamboanga Peninsula (geographical region)">
        Zamboanga
       </a>
       down to
       <a href="/wiki/Tawi-Tawi" title="Tawi-Tawi">
        Tawi-Tawi
       </a>
       among a minority of the
       <a href="/wiki/Taus%C5%ABg_people" title="Tausūg people">
        Tausug
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sama-Bajau" title="Sama-Bajau">
        Sama-Bajau
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yakan" title="Yakan">
        Yakan
       </a>
       peoples. It is also spoken as a daily language by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malay_people" title="Malay people">
        Malays
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
        Indonesians
       </a>
       who have settled, or do business in the Philippines. It is also spoken in southern
       <a href="/wiki/Palawan" title="Palawan">
        Palawan
       </a>
       to some extent. It is not spoken among the
       <a href="/wiki/Maranao_people" title="Maranao people">
        Maranao
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Maguindanao_people" title="Maguindanao people">
        Maguindanao
       </a>
       people. Malaysia, Indonesia, and the southern Philippines are largely Islamic and the liturgical language of Islam is
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
        Arabic
       </a>
       , but the vast majority of Muslims in the Philippines have little practical knowledge of Arabic beyond limited religious terminology.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Malay_language" title="Old Malay language">
        Old Malay
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       cultures and civilizations in ancient Sumatra and Java influenced the history, lifestyles, and culture of Filipino peoples. The
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
        Malay language
       </a>
       , along with Philippine languages belonging to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian" title="Malayo-Polynesian">
        Malayo-Polynesian
       </a>
       language family, has also had an immense influence on many if not most of the languages spoken in the Philippines. Roughly a third of all commonly used verbs and nouns used in the Philippines are of Old Malay origin. This is because Old Malay used to be the lingua franca throughout the archipelago, a good example of this is Magellan's translator Enrique using Malay to converse with the native
       <i>
        Sugbuanon
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Cebuano_language" title="Cebuano language">
        Cebuano
       </a>
       ) during this time period.
      </p>
      <p>
       An example of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Malay_language" title="Old Malay language">
        Old Malay
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Javanese_language" title="Javanese language">
        Javanese
       </a>
       languages spoken in Philippine history can be seen in the language of the 10th century
       <a href="/wiki/Laguna_Copperplate_Inscription" title="Laguna Copperplate Inscription">
        Laguna Copperplate Inscription
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       When the Spanish had first arrived in the Philippines in the 16th century, Old Malay was spoken among the
       <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">
        aristocracy
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It is believed that
       <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">
        Ferdinand Magellan
       </a>
       ’s Moluccan slave
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_the_Black" title="Henry the Black">
        Enrique
       </a>
       could converse with local leaders in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cebu_Island" title="Cebu Island">
        Cebu
       </a>
       island, confirming to Magellan his arrival in Southeast Asia.
      </p>
      <p>
       Today,
       <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">
        Indonesian
       </a>
       is taught as a foreign language in the Department of Linguistics and Asian Languages in the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_Philippines" title="University of the Philippines">
        University of the Philippines
       </a>
       . Also, the Indonesian School in
       <a href="/wiki/Davao_City" title="Davao City">
        Davao City
       </a>
       teaches the language to preserve the culture of Indonesian immigrants there. The Indonesian Embassy in Manila also offers occasional classes for Filipinos and foreigners.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 2013, the Indonesian Embassy in the Philippines has given basic Indonesian language training to members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_the_Philippines" title="Armed Forces of the Philippines">
        Armed Forces of the Philippines
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In an interview,
       <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Education_(Philippines)" title="Department of Education (Philippines)">
        Department of Education
       </a>
       Secretary
       <a href="/wiki/Armin_Luistro" title="Armin Luistro">
        Armin Luistro
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       said that the country's government should promote Indonesian or Malay, which are related to Filipino. Thus, the possibility of offering it as an optional subject in public schools is being studied.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="South_Asian_languages">
        South Asian languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: South Asian languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since pre-Spanish times, there have been small Indian communities in the Philippines. Indians tend to be able to speak Tagalog and the other native languages, and are often fluent in English. Among themselves,
       <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">
        Sindhi
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">
        Punjabi
       </a>
       are used.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language" title="Urdu language">
        Urdu
       </a>
       is spoken among the
       <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">
        Pakistani
       </a>
       community. Only few South Asians, such as Pakistani, as well as the recent newcomers like speakers of
       <a href="/wiki/Tamil_language" title="Tamil language">
        Tamil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">
        Nepali
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Marathi_language" title="Marathi language">
        Marathi
       </a>
       retain their own respective languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-almanac_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-almanac-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish">
        Spanish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Spanish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines" title="Spanish language in the Philippines">
        Spanish language in the Philippines
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language_in_the_Philippines" title="Spanish language in the Philippines">
        Spanish
       </a>
       was introduced in the islands after 1565, when the Spanish
       <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">
        conquistador
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Miguel_L%C3%B3pez_de_Legazpi" title="Miguel López de Legazpi">
        Miguel López de Legazpi
       </a>
       set sail from
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       and founded the first Spanish settlement on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceb%C3%BA" title="Cebú">
        Cebú
       </a>
       . However, it is rarely spoken today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SDO2009-01-21_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SDO2009-01-21-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1593, the first printing press in the Philippine islands was founded and it released the first (albeit polyglot) book, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Doctrina_Christiana" title="Doctrina Christiana">
         Doctrina Christiana
        </a>
       </i>
       that same year. In the 17th century, Spanish religious orders founded the first universities in the Philippines, some of which are considered the oldest in Asia. During colonial rule through
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       , Spanish was the language of education, trade, politics, and religion, and by the 19th century, became the colony's
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       although it was mainly used by the educated Filipinos.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1863, a Spanish decree introduced a system of public education, creating free public schooling in Spanish. In the 1890s, the Philippines had a prominent group of Spanish-speaking scholars called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilustrados" title="Ilustrados">
        Ilustrados
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Rizal" title="José Rizal">
        José Rizal
       </a>
       . Some of these scholars participated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Revolution" title="Philippine Revolution">
        Philippine Revolution
       </a>
       and later in the struggle against American occupation. Both the
       <a href="/wiki/Malolos_Constitution" title="Malolos Constitution">
        Malolos Constitution
       </a>
       and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Lupang_Hinirang" title="Lupang Hinirang">
         Lupang Hinirang
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">
        national anthem
       </a>
       ) were written in Spanish.
      </p>
      <p>
       Under U.S. rule, the
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English language
       </a>
       began to be promoted instead of Spanish. The use of Spanish began to decline some years after Spain was forced to pass the islands to the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       as a result of the introduction of English into the public schools as a language of instruction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhilCS-Edu_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PhilCS-Edu-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1950 census stated that Filipinos who spoke Spanish as a first or second language made up only 6% of the population. In 1990, the census reported that the number had dwindled to just 2,500. A 2012 survey estimates that while around 3 million people can speak Spanish with varying degrees of competency, only around 439,000 people can speak the language at a native level.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Spanish briefly lost its status as an official language in the 1973 constitution but regained official status two months later when President Marcos signed Presidential Decree No. 155.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD_No._155_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-PD_No._155-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With the promulgation of the 1987 constitution, Spanish lost its official status and it was dropped as a college requirement during
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coraz%C3%B3n_Aquino" title="Corazón Aquino">
        Corazón Aquino
       </a>
       's administration. Former president
       <a href="/wiki/Gloria_Macapagal_Arroyo" title="Gloria Macapagal Arroyo">
        Gloria Macapagal Arroyo
       </a>
       , a third-language Spanish speaker, introduced legislation to re-establish the instruction of Spanish in 2009 in the state education system. Today, the language is still spoken by Filipino-Spanish mestizos and Spanish families who are mainly concentrated in Metro Manila,
       <a href="/wiki/Iloilo_City" title="Iloilo City">
        Iloilo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cebu_City" title="Cebu City">
        Cebu
       </a>
       . It remains a required subject in some academic institutions, such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Santo_Tom%C3%A1s" title="University of Santo Tomás">
        University of Santo Tomás
       </a>
       in Manila and the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_San_Carlos" title="University of San Carlos">
        University of San Carlos
       </a>
       in Cebu. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1987_Constitution_of_the_Philippines" title="1987 Constitution of the Philippines">
        1987 Constitution
       </a>
       mandates that Spanish (along with Arabic) is to be promoted on an optional and voluntary basis.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many historical documents, land titles, and works of literature are written in Spanish and are still not translated into Filipino languages, despite the fact that some such as land titles have legal value. Spanish, through colonization has contributed the largest number of
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_loanwords_in_Tagalog#Spanish" title="List of loanwords in Tagalog">
        loanwords and expressions
       </a>
       in Tagalog, Cebuano, and other Philippine languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_creoles">
        Spanish creoles
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Languages_of_the_Philippines&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Spanish creoles">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are several
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish-based_creole_languages" title="Spanish-based creole languages">
        Spanish-based creole languages
       </a>
       in the Philippines, collectively called
       <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
        Chavacano
       </a>
       . These may be split into two major geographical groups:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        In Luzon:
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano#Caviteño_/_Ternateño" title="Chavacano">
            Caviteño
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Cavite), spoken in
          <a href="/wiki/Cavite_City" title="Cavite City">
           Cavite City
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Cavite" title="Cavite">
           Cavite
          </a>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano#Caviteño_/_Ternateño" title="Chavacano">
            Ternateño
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Barra), spoken in
          <a href="/wiki/Ternate,_Cavite" title="Ternate, Cavite">
           Ternate
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Cavite" title="Cavite">
           Cavite
          </a>
          .
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano#Ermitaño" title="Chavacano">
            Ermitaño
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Ermita), formerly spoken in
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ermita,_Manila" title="Ermita, Manila">
           Ermita, Manila
          </a>
          but is now
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Language_extinction" title="Language extinction">
           extinct
          </a>
          . The last reported speakers were a woman and her grandson during the 1980s and 1990s.
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        In Mindanao:
        <ul>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
            Zamboangueño Chavacano
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Zamboanga / Zamboangueño Chavacano), spoken in
          <a href="/wiki/Zamboanga_City" title="Zamboanga City">
           Zamboanga City
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Zamboanga_Sibugay" title="Zamboanga Sibugay">
           Zamboanga Sibugay
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Zamboanga_del_Sur" title="Zamboanga del Sur">
           Zamboanga del Sur
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Zamboanga_del_Norte" title="Zamboanga del Norte">
           Zamboanga del Norte
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Basilan" title="Basilan">
           Basilan
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Sulu" title="Sulu">
           Sulu
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Tawi-Tawi" title="Tawi-Tawi">
           Tawi-Tawi
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/Semporna" title="Semporna">
           Semporna
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">
           Sabah
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">
           Malaysia
          </a>
          (360,000 native speakers-Zamboanga City alone as per 2000 census, making it the most spoken form and known form of Chavacano)
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
            Cotabateño
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Cotabato), spoken in
          <a href="/wiki/Cotabato" title="Cotabato">
           Cotabato
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Chavacano" title="Chavacano">
            Davaoeño Abakay
           </a>
          </i>
          (Chabacano de Davao), spoken in
          <a href="/wiki/Davao_City" title="Davao City">
           Davao City
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">

      </div>

       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-philconst-1">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

          <a href="#cite_ref-philconst_1-0">
           <sup>
            <i>
             <b>

             </b>
            </i>
           </sup>
          </a>

             </b>
            </i>

         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">

            "The Constitution of the Republic of the Philippines - GOVPH"
           </a>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Philippines&amp;rft.pub=Ethnologue&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ethnologue.com%2Fcountry%2Fph%2Flanguages%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALanguages+of+the+Philippines">

        <li id="cite_note-9">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-9">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <b>
           Article XIV, Sec 7
          </b>
          : For purposes of communication and instruction, the official languages of the Philippines are Filipino and, until otherwise provided by law, English. The regional languages are the auxiliary official languages in the regions and shall serve as auxiliary media of instruction therein. Spanish and Arabic shall be promoted on a voluntary and optional basis.
         </span>

          </a>
